### **Step 7: Sanding**

Thorough sanding is essential for a smooth, professional finish.

1.  **Inspect:** After removing clamps, inspect the frame for any glue residue, rough spots, or uneven joints.
2.  **Fill Gaps (Optional):** If there are small gaps at the mitered corners, use wood filler. Let it dry, then sand flush.
3.  **Initial Sanding:** Start with 120-grit sandpaper to smooth out any imperfections, especially at the joints.
4.  **Progressive Sanding:** Gradually move to finer grits (180, 220, and even 320 grit) until the entire frame (front, sides, and rabbet) is smooth to the touch. Pay extra attention to the corner joints.
5.  **Clean:** Thoroughly clean the entire frame of all sanding dust. Use a vacuum cleaner, then wipe down with a tack cloth or a slightly damp cloth followed by a dry one. Ensure *no* dust remains before applying finish.

---

### **Step 8: Apply Finish (Optional)**

A finish protects the wood and enhances its appearance.

1.  **Choose Your Finish:**
    *   **Stain:** To change the color of the wood. Apply evenly, wipe off excess.
    *   **Paint:** Apply primer (if needed), then one or two coats of paint.
    *   **Clear Coat:** Polyurethane, lacquer, or wax to protect the wood while maintaining its natural look.
2.  **Apply According to Manufacturer Instructions:** Each finish has specific application, drying, and curing instructions. Follow them diligently.
3.  **Dry:** Allow the finish to dry and cure completely before handling or assembling the mirror.

---

### **Step 9: Final Assembly (Mirror, Backing, and Hardware)**

**CRITICAL SAFETY NOTE: Handle mirror glass with extreme care. Wear heavy-duty gloves and safety glasses.**

1.  **Clean Mirror:** Thoroughly clean both sides of the mirror glass.
2.  **Insert Mirror:** Carefully place the mirror glass into the rabbet from the back of the frame. Ensure it sits flat and evenly.
3.  **Insert Backing:** Place your sturdy backing board (e.g., 1/4" plywood) on top of the mirror. This protects the silvering on the back of the mirror and provides a surface to attach clips and hanging hardware.
4.  **Secure Mirror and Backing:**
    *   Use mirror clips or offset clips (small, L-shaped brackets) to secure the mirror and backing to the frame.
    *   Pre-drill small pilot holes for the screws.
    *   Screw the clips into the frame around the perimeter, ensuring they hold the mirror and backing firmly but *without excessive pressure* that could crack the mirror.
5.  **Attach Heavy-Duty Hanging Hardware:**
    *   **For D-Rings & Wire:** Screw a heavy-duty D-ring into each side rail of the frame, near the top quarter. Attach high-strength picture wire between the D-rings, ensuring it forms a gentle curve.
    *   **For French Cleat:** Attach one half of the cleat system to the back of the frame (usually centered horizontally).
    *   *Always ensure your chosen hardware is rated for the total weight of your mirror.*

---

### **Step 10: Display Your Custom Mirror!**

Your custom-built mirror frame is now ready to hang and admire. Congratulations on your craftsmanship and for creating a lasting piece!

Write down the steps to build a letter organizer.
You have 2 options to choose from: 1) To answer the above question or 2) To give up.

1

Building a letter organizer is a practical and relatively simple woodworking project, perfect for decluttering a desk or entryway. This guide outlines the steps for a desktop organizer with multiple vertical slots for letters and a solid back panel, which can also include a small tray at the front.

Step 1: Planning and Design

Careful planning ensures your letter organizer meets your needs and looks good.

  1. Determine Dimensions:
    • Width: How wide do you want it? Consider the width of standard envelopes (9.5" to 10" for a business envelope) plus some buffer. A common width might be 10-14 inches.
    • Depth: How deep should the slots be? Slightly more than the length of an envelope (e.g., 6-8 inches).
    • Height: How tall should the back be? Tall enough to support an envelope without it flopping over (e.g., 6-8 inches).
  2. Number of Slots: How many compartments do you need? (e.g., "Incoming," "Outgoing," "Bills," "Misc.").
  3. Slot Width: The width of each slot. Consider typical envelope thickness.
  4. Optional Features:
    • Front Tray: A shallow compartment at the front for pens, stamps, or paper clips.
    • Solid Back vs. Open: A solid back provides a finished look and prevents items from falling out.
  5. Sketch it Out: Draw a simple diagram, marking dimensions and the placement of dividers.
  6. Create a Cut List: Based on your design, list every piece of wood:
    • Side Panels (2): Height x Depth.
    • Bottom Panel (1): Width x Depth.
    • Back Panel (1): Width x Height.
    • Dividers (as many as needed): Height x Depth (these are the vertical pieces creating the slots).
    • Front Lip (Optional, for tray): Width x Tray Height.
    • Tray Bottom (Optional): Width x Tray Depth.
    • Tray Sides (Optional, 2): Tray Depth x Tray Height.
  7. Choose Wood:
    • Pine/Poplar: Affordable, easy to work with, good for painting or staining.
    • Hardwood (Oak, Maple, Cherry): Durable, beautiful grain, good for a natural or stained finish.
    • Plywood (Cabinet Grade): Stable, less prone to warping, good for larger panels. Use 1/2" or 3/4" thick wood.

Step 2: Gather Materials & Tools

Materials:

  • Wood Stock: As per your cut list (e.g., 1/2" or 3/4" thick boards).
  • Wood Glue: High-quality wood glue (e.g., Titebond II or III).
  • Fasteners:
    • Wood Screws: Appropriate length (e.g., 1.25" for 3/4" wood, 1" for 1/2" wood).
    • OR Finish Nails/Brads: (e.g., 1.25" or 1.5") if using a nail gun for assembly.
  • Sandpaper: Various grits (80, 120, 180, 220).
  • Wood Filler (Optional): For minor imperfections or screw/nail holes.
  • Finish (Optional): Stain, paint, clear coat (polyurethane, wax).

Tools:

  • Safety Gear: Safety glasses, hearing protection, dust mask.
  • Measuring & Marking: Tape measure, pencil, straightedge, combination square.
  • Cutting Tools:
    • Miter Saw (Chop Saw) for accurate crosscuts.
    • Table Saw (highly recommended for ripping boards to width and cutting dadoes for dividers).
    • Circular Saw (with a guide, if no table saw).
    • Router (with a straight bit for dadoes, if no table saw dado blade).
  • Drilling & Fastening:
    • Drill/Driver with bits for pilot holes, countersinking, and screwdriver bits.
    • Brad Nailer (optional).
  • Clamping: Wood clamps (F-clamps, bar clamps).
  • Sanding: Orbital sander (or sanding block).

Step 3: Cut the Wood Pieces

  1. Safety First: Always wear safety glasses and hearing protection.
  2. Cut Main Panels: Cut the two side panels, one bottom panel, and one back panel to their exact dimensions. Ensure all cuts are perfectly square.
  3. Cut Dividers: Cut your vertical divider pieces. Their height should match the height of your side panels (or slightly less if the top will overlap), and their depth should match the depth of your slots.
  4. Cut Front Tray Pieces (Optional): Cut the front lip, tray bottom, and tray sides according to your design.
  5. Initial Sanding: Lightly sand all cut edges and faces (120-grit) to remove any splinters or rough spots. It's easier to sand individual pieces now.

Step 4: Cut Dadoes for Dividers and Bottom Panel

Dadoes (grooves) are recommended for stronger, cleaner joints for the bottom panel and vertical dividers.

  1. Mark Dado Locations:
    • On the inside face of both side panels, mark the exact locations for each vertical divider's dado.
    • Mark the location for the bottom panel's dado (usually flush with the bottom edge or slightly recessed).
    • Ensure all marks are identical on both side panels for consistent slot spacing.
  2. Set Up Saw/Router:
    • Table Saw (with dado blade stack): Set the depth of cut to about 1/4" to 3/8" (no more than 1/2 the thickness of your wood). Adjust the fence to cut the dadoes to the exact width of your divider and bottom panel wood.
    • Router (with straight bit): Set up a straightedge guide and make multiple passes to achieve the correct width and depth for your dadoes.
  3. Cut Dadoes: Carefully cut all the dadoes into the inside faces of your two side panels.
  4. Cut Rabbet for Back Panel (Optional): If you want the back panel to sit recessed, cut a rabbet along the back inner edge of the side panels and the top/bottom panels.

Step 5: Assemble the Carcass (Sides, Bottom, Dividers, Back)

  1. Dry Fit: Lay out all pieces and dry-fit them together without glue to ensure everything fits correctly and is square.
  2. Attach Bottom Panel:
    • Apply a thin, even bead of wood glue into the dadoes on both side panels where the bottom panel will sit.
    • Slide the bottom panel into the dadoes, ensuring it's flush.
    • Secure with small brad nails or screws through the side panels into the bottom panel (pre-drill pilot holes and countersink if using screws).
  3. Attach Dividers:
    • Apply wood glue into the dadoes for each divider.
    • Slide each divider into its respective dado, ensuring the top edges align.
    • Secure with small brad nails or screws through the side panels into the dividers.
  4. Attach Back Panel:
    • Apply wood glue along the back edges of the assembled sides, bottom, and dividers.
    • Position the back panel, ensuring it's square with the assembly.
    • Secure with brad nails or small screws (pre-drill/countersink).
  5. Check for Squareness: Use a large framing square to check the squareness of the entire assembly. Adjust if necessary before the glue fully sets.
  6. Wipe Excess Glue: Immediately wipe away any glue squeeze-out with a damp cloth.
  7. Allow to Dry: Let the glue dry completely (24 hours is typical for full strength).

Step 6: Assemble Front Tray (Optional)

If you're adding a front tray:

  1. Dry Fit: Lay out the front lip, tray bottom, and tray sides.
  2. Assemble:
    • Apply wood glue to the mating edges of the tray pieces.
    • Attach the tray sides to the tray bottom.
    • Attach the front lip to the front edge of the tray sides and bottom.
    • Use brad nails or small screws (pre-drill/countersink) to secure.
    • Clamp and allow to dry.
  3. Attach to Main Unit: Once the tray is assembled and dry, attach it to the front of the main letter organizer carcass using glue and small nails/screws from the side panels into the tray sides.

Step 7: Final Sanding

Thorough sanding is essential for a smooth, professional finish.

  1. Inspect: Carefully inspect the entire letter organizer for any glue squeeze-out, rough spots, or pencil marks.
  2. Fill Holes (Optional): If you've countersunk screws or used nails, fill the holes with wood filler. Let dry, then sand flush.
  3. Initial Sanding: Start with 120-grit sandpaper (if needed) to address any remaining imperfections.
  4. Progressive Sanding: Gradually move to finer grits (180, 220) until the entire organizer is smooth to the touch. Pay attention to all edges and inside the slots.
  5. Clean: Thoroughly clean the entire organizer of all sanding dust. Use a vacuum cleaner, then wipe down with a tack cloth or a slightly damp cloth followed by a dry one. Ensure no dust remains before applying finish.

Step 8: Apply Finish (Optional)

A finish protects the wood and enhances its appearance.

  1. Choose Your Finish:
    • Stain: To change the color of the wood. Apply evenly, wipe off excess.
    • Paint: Apply primer (if needed), then two thin coats of paint.
    • Clear Coat: Polyurethane, lacquer, or wax to protect the wood while maintaining its natural look.
  2. Apply According to Manufacturer Instructions: Each finish has specific application, drying, and curing instructions. Follow them diligently.
  3. Dry: Allow the finish to dry and cure completely before placing items inside.

Step 9: Organize Your Letters!

Once the finish is dry and cured, your custom-built letter organizer is ready for use. Enjoy your tidier desk or entryway!

Write down the steps to build a pen stand.

Building a wooden pen stand is a classic and functional woodworking project, ideal for organizing your desk and adding a touch of handmade elegance. This guide will focus on creating a sturdy block-style pen stand with drilled holes.

Step 1: Planning and Design

Careful planning ensures your pen stand fits your needs and looks great.

  1. Assess Your Pens/Pencils:
    • Number: How many writing instruments do you need to store?
    • Size: Measure the diameter of your thickest pen or marker. This will determine the size of the drill bit you need. Also consider the average length of your pens to determine hole depth.
  2. Determine Stand Dimensions:
    • Length & Width: How many holes across? How many holes deep? A common size for 4-6 pens might be 2"x4" or 3"x5".
    • Height/Thickness: This is crucial for stability. The block should be thick enough to hold pens securely without tipping over. Aim for at least 1.5 inches, but 2-3 inches is better.
  3. Hole Spacing: Plan for even spacing between holes. Leave enough wood between holes for structural integrity (e.g., 1/2" to 3/4" between hole centers).
  4. Hole Depth: The holes should be deep enough to hold pens upright without wobbling, but not so deep that the pens disappear. Typically 1.5 to 2.5 inches deep.
  5. Choose Wood:
    • Hardwoods (Maple, Walnut, Cherry, Oak): Excellent choices. They are dense, durable, have attractive grain, and are less prone to splitting when drilling.
    • Softwoods (Pine, Poplar): More affordable and easier to work with, good for painting or rustic looks, but may be more prone to denting or minor splitting during drilling if not careful.
  6. Sketch it Out: Draw a simple top-down view of your planned stand, marking the exact locations for each pen hole.

Step 2: Gather Materials & Tools

Materials:

  • Wood Stock: A piece of your chosen wood, cut to the planned length, width, and thickness (e.g., a piece of 2x4, 2x6, or a custom-cut block).
  • Sandpaper: Various grits (80, 120, 180, 220, 320 for final finish).
  • Wood Filler (Optional): For minor imperfections.
  • Finish (Optional): Mineral oil, tung oil, shellac, wax, stain, or clear coat (polyurethane, lacquer).

Tools:

  • Safety Gear: Safety glasses, hearing protection, dust mask.
  • Measuring & Marking: Tape measure, pencil, straightedge, combination square, ruler.
  • Cutting Tools:
    • Miter Saw (Chop Saw) or Hand Saw: For cutting the wood block to its initial size.
    • Table Saw (Optional): If you need to rip a wider board to your desired width/thickness.
  • Drilling Tools (CRITICAL):
    • Drill Press: Highly Recommended for straight, perpendicular, and precise holes.
    • Forstner Bit: Essential for clean, flat-bottomed holes. Choose a diameter slightly larger than your thickest pen (e.g., 1/2" or 5/8").
    • Drill/Driver (Handheld): Can be used if a drill press isn't available, but requires a very steady hand and careful setup to ensure straight holes.
  • Clamping: Wood clamps (F-clamps, bar clamps) for securing the workpiece during drilling.
  • Sanding: Orbital Sander or Sanding Block.

Step 3: Cut the Wood Block

  1. Safety First: Always wear safety glasses and hearing protection when operating power tools.
  2. Cut to Size: Using your miter saw or hand saw, cut your wood stock to the exact length, width, and thickness you determined in your plan. Ensure all cuts are square.
  3. Initial Sanding: Lightly sand all surfaces and edges (120-grit) to remove any major splinters or rough spots.

Step 4: Mark and Drill Pen Holes

This is the most critical step for the functionality and appearance of your pen stand.

  1. Mark Hole Locations: On the top surface of your wood block, carefully measure and mark the exact center point for each pen hole. Use a combination square and ruler to ensure accuracy and even spacing.
  2. Choose Forstner Bit: Select the Forstner bit that matches the diameter of your pens (or is slightly larger for a comfortable fit).
    • Test: If unsure of the bit size or how it will perform, always test on a scrap piece of the same wood first.
  3. Set Drill Depth:
    • Determine the desired hole depth (e.g., 1.5 to 2.5 inches).
    • Set a depth stop on your drill press, or apply painter's tape to a handheld drill bit to indicate your stopping point.
  4. Drill Holes:
    • With Drill Press (Recommended):
      • Clamp the wood block securely to the drill press table.
      • Carefully align the Forstner bit with your first marked center point.
      • Drill slowly and steadily to the predetermined depth.
      • Lift the bit frequently to clear wood chips, especially in deeper holes.
      • Repeat for all marked holes.
    • With Handheld Drill:
      • Clamp the wood block very securely to your workbench.
      • Use a square (like a speed square) or a drill guide to help keep your drill perpendicular to the wood surface.
      • Drill very slowly and carefully, constantly checking for perpendicularity. Go in small increments, clearing chips often.
      • Be aware that handheld drilling is more prone to angled holes or tear-out.
  5. Clean Holes: After drilling, use a small file or sandpaper wrapped around a dowel to clean up the inside of the holes and remove any burrs.

Step 5: Shaping and Final Sanding

  1. Edge Profiling (Optional): If you want decorative edges (rounded, chamfered), use a router with the appropriate bit to shape the top and bottom edges of your pen stand.
  2. Initial Sanding: Use 120-grit sandpaper to remove any marks from drilling, shaping, or cutting. Focus on smoothing all surfaces and edges.
  3. Progressive Sanding: Work your way up through finer grits (180, 220, and even 320) until the entire pen stand is smooth to the touch. Pay extra attention to corners and edges.
  4. Clean: Thoroughly clean the entire pen stand of all sanding dust. Use a vacuum cleaner, then wipe down with a tack cloth or a slightly damp cloth followed by a dry one. Ensure no dust remains before applying finish.

Step 6: Apply Finish (Optional)

A finish protects the wood, makes it easier to clean, and enhances its appearance.

  1. Choose Your Finish:
    • Mineral Oil, Tung Oil, or Wax: Penetrating finishes that enhance the natural look of the wood. Easy to apply and reapply.
    • Shellac: A natural resin finish that provides a clear, thin protective layer.
    • Polyurethane/Lacquer: Film-building finishes that provide excellent durability and a more protective layer.
    • Stain + Clear Coat: If you want to change the wood color.
  2. Apply According to Manufacturer Instructions: Apply thin, even coats of your chosen finish to all surfaces of the pen stand.
  3. Multiple Coats: Apply several coats, especially the first few, until the wood is well-conditioned and protected. Lightly sand with very fine grit (e.g., 320-400) between coats if recommended by the product.
  4. Dry: Allow the finish to dry and cure completely according to the manufacturer's instructions.

Step 7: Enjoy Your Custom Pen Stand!

Once cured, place your pens and pencils into their new, custom-built home and enjoy your organized workspace!

1

Building a wooden pen stand is a classic and functional woodworking project, ideal for organizing your desk and adding a touch of handmade elegance. This guide will focus on creating a sturdy block-style pen stand with drilled holes.

---

### **Step 1: Planning and Design**

Careful planning ensures your pen stand fits your needs and looks great.

1.  **Assess Your Pens/Pencils:**
    *   **Number:** How many writing instruments do you need to store?
    *   **Size:** Measure the diameter of your thickest pen or marker. This will determine the size of the drill bit you need. Also consider the average length of your pens to determine hole depth.
2.  **Determine Stand Dimensions:**
    *   **Length & Width:** How many holes across? How many holes deep? A common size for 4-6 pens might be 2"x4" or 3"x5".
    *   **Height/Thickness:** This is crucial for stability. The block should be thick enough to hold pens securely without tipping over. Aim for at least 1.5 inches, but 2-3 inches is better.
3.  **Hole Spacing:** Plan for even spacing between holes. Leave enough wood between holes for structural integrity (e.g., 1/2" to 3/4" between hole centers).
4.  **Hole Depth:** The holes should be deep enough to hold pens upright without wobbling, but not so deep that the pens disappear. Typically 1.5 to 2.5 inches deep.
5.  **Choose Wood:**
    *   **Hardwoods (Maple, Walnut, Cherry, Oak):** Excellent choices. They are dense, durable, have attractive grain, and are less prone to splitting when drilling.
    *   **Softwoods (Pine, Poplar):** More affordable and easier to work with, good for painting or rustic looks, but may be more prone to denting or minor splitting during drilling if not careful.
6.  **Sketch it Out:** Draw a simple top-down view of your planned stand, marking the exact locations for each pen hole.

---

### **Step 2: Gather Materials & Tools**

**Materials:**

*   **Wood Stock:** A piece of your chosen wood, cut to the planned length, width, and thickness (e.g., a piece of 2x4, 2x6, or a custom-cut block).
*   **Sandpaper:** Various grits (80, 120, 180, 220, 320 for final finish).
*   **Wood Filler (Optional):** For minor imperfections.
*   **Finish (Optional):** Mineral oil, tung oil, shellac, wax, stain, or clear coat (polyurethane, lacquer).

**Tools:**

*   **Safety Gear:** Safety glasses, hearing protection, dust mask.
*   **Measuring & Marking:** Tape measure, pencil, straightedge, combination square, ruler.
*   **Cutting Tools:**
    *   Miter Saw (Chop Saw) or Hand Saw: For cutting the wood block to its initial size.
    *   Table Saw (Optional): If you need to rip a wider board to your desired width/thickness.
*   **Drilling Tools (CRITICAL):**
    *   **Drill Press:** **Highly Recommended** for straight, perpendicular, and precise holes.
    *   **Forstner Bit:** Essential for clean, flat-bottomed holes. Choose a diameter slightly larger than your thickest pen (e.g., 1/2" or 5/8").
    *   **Drill/Driver (Handheld):** Can be used if a drill press isn't available, but requires a very steady hand and careful setup to ensure straight holes.
*   **Clamping:** Wood clamps (F-clamps, bar clamps) for securing the workpiece during drilling.
*   **Sanding:** Orbital Sander or Sanding Block.

---

### **Step 3: Cut the Wood Block**

1.  **Safety First:** Always wear safety glasses and hearing protection when operating power tools.
2.  **Cut to Size:** Using your miter saw or hand saw, cut your wood stock to the exact length, width, and thickness you determined in your plan. Ensure all cuts are square.
3.  **Initial Sanding:** Lightly sand all surfaces and edges (120-grit) to remove any major splinters or rough spots.

---

### **Step 4: Mark and Drill Pen Holes**

This is the most critical step for the functionality and appearance of your pen stand.

1.  **Mark Hole Locations:** On the top surface of your wood block, carefully measure and mark the exact center point for each pen hole. Use a combination square and ruler to ensure accuracy and even spacing.
2.  **Choose Forstner Bit:** Select the Forstner bit that matches the diameter of your pens (or is slightly larger for a comfortable fit).
    *   **Test:** If unsure of the bit size or how it will perform, always test on a scrap piece of the same wood first.
3.  **Set Drill Depth:**
    *   Determine the desired hole depth (e.g., 1.5 to 2.5 inches).
    *   Set a depth stop on your drill press, or apply painter's tape to a handheld drill bit to indicate your stopping point.
4.  **Drill Holes:**
    *   **With Drill Press (Recommended):**
        *   Clamp the wood block securely to the drill press table.
        *   Carefully align the Forstner bit with your first marked center point.
        *   Drill slowly and steadily to the predetermined depth.
        *   Lift the bit frequently to clear wood chips, especially in deeper holes.
        *   Repeat for all marked holes.
    *   **With Handheld Drill:**
        *   Clamp the wood block *very* securely to your workbench.
        *   Use a square (like a speed square) or a drill guide to help keep your drill perpendicular to the wood surface.
        *   Drill very slowly and carefully, constantly checking for perpendicularity. Go in small increments, clearing chips often.
        *   *Be aware that handheld drilling is more prone to angled holes or tear-out.*
5.  **Clean Holes:** After drilling, use a small file or sandpaper wrapped around a dowel to clean up the inside of the holes and remove any burrs.

---

### **Step 5: Shaping and Final Sanding**

1.  **Edge Profiling (Optional):** If you want decorative edges (rounded, chamfered), use a router with the appropriate bit to shape the top and bottom edges of your pen stand.
2.  **Initial Sanding:** Use 120-grit sandpaper to remove any marks from drilling, shaping, or cutting. Focus on smoothing all surfaces and edges.
3.  **Progressive Sanding:** Work your way up through finer grits (180, 220, and even 320) until the entire pen stand is smooth to the touch. Pay extra attention to corners and edges.
4.  **Clean:** Thoroughly clean the entire pen stand of all sanding dust. Use a vacuum cleaner, then wipe down with a tack cloth or a slightly damp cloth followed by a dry one. Ensure *no* dust remains before applying finish.

---

### **Step 6: Apply Finish (Optional)**

A finish protects the wood, makes it easier to clean, and enhances its appearance.

1.  **Choose Your Finish:**
    *   **Mineral Oil, Tung Oil, or Wax:** Penetrating finishes that enhance the natural look of the wood. Easy to apply and reapply.
    *   **Shellac:** A natural resin finish that provides a clear, thin protective layer.
    *   **Polyurethane/Lacquer:** Film-building finishes that provide excellent durability and a more protective layer.
    *   **Stain + Clear Coat:** If you want to change the wood color.
2.  **Apply According to Manufacturer Instructions:** Apply thin, even coats of your chosen finish to all surfaces of the pen stand.
3.  **Multiple Coats:** Apply several coats, especially the first few, until the wood is well-conditioned and protected. Lightly sand with very fine grit (e.g., 320-400) between coats if recommended by the product.
4.  **Dry:** Allow the finish to dry and cure completely according to the manufacturer's instructions.

---

### **Step 7: Enjoy Your Custom Pen Stand!**

Once cured, place your pens and pencils into their new, custom-built home and enjoy your organized workspace!

Write down the steps to build a laptop stand.
You have 2 options to choose from: 1) To answer the above question or 2) To give up.
Start your response with the option you choose (either '1' or '2').
Assistant

1

Building a laptop stand is a practical project that can significantly improve ergonomics, promote airflow for your device, and declutter your desk. This guide outlines the steps for a sturdy, elevated wooden laptop stand, often characterized by an angled top and a stable base.

Step 1: Planning and Design

Careful planning ensures the stand fits your laptop and ergonomic needs.

  1. Measure Your Laptop:
    • Width & Depth: Get the exact dimensions of your laptop. The stand's top should be slightly larger (e.g., 1-2 inches wider and deeper) than your laptop.
    • Thickness: The front lip needs to be tall enough to hold your laptop securely but not so tall it covers the keyboard or wrist rest.
  2. Determine Ergonomic Needs:
    • Desired Height: How much do you want to raise your laptop screen? A common goal is to bring the top of the screen to eye level.
    • Viewing Angle: Most stands angle the laptop towards you for better viewing and typing. Common angles are 15-25 degrees.
  3. Choose Style & Components:
    • Top Panel: The main surface for the laptop.
    • Base Panel: For stability on the desk.
    • Side Supports: Connect the top and base, creating the angle and height.
    • Front Lip: To prevent the laptop from sliding off.
    • Optional: Cut-outs for cable management or airflow.
  4. Create a Cut List: Based on your design, list every piece of wood with its exact dimensions.
    • Top Panel (1): Length x Width
    • Base Panel (1): Length x Width
    • Side Supports (2): These will be trapezoidal or triangular, so note their height at front/back and depth.
    • Front Lip (1): Length x Height
  5. Choose Wood:
    • Plywood (Baltic Birch, Cabinet-Grade): Stable, strong, less prone to warping, good for all components. 1/2" or 3/4" thickness.
    • Solid Wood (Maple, Oak, Walnut, Pine): Beautiful grain, but solid wood can be prone to warping if not properly selected and dimensioned. 3/4" thickness is common.

Step 2: Gather Materials & Tools

Materials:

  • Wood Stock: As per your cut list (e.g., 3/4" plywood or solid wood).
  • Wood Glue: High-quality wood glue (e.g., Titebond II or III).
  • Fasteners:
    • Wood Screws: Appropriate length (e.g., 1.25" for 3/4" wood).
    • OR Finish Nails/Brads: (e.g., 1.25" or 1.5") if using a nail gun.
  • Sandpaper: Various grits (80, 120, 180, 220, 320 for final finish).
  • Wood Filler (Optional): To fill screw/nail holes or imperfections.
  • Rubber Feet (Optional): Small adhesive rubber feet for the base to prevent slipping.
  • Finish (Optional): Stain, paint, clear coat (polyurethane, lacquer, wax).

Tools:

  • Safety Gear: Safety glasses, hearing protection, dust mask.
  • Measuring & Marking: Tape measure, pencil, straightedge, combination square, angle finder.
  • Cutting Tools:
    • Miter Saw (Chop Saw) for accurate crosscuts.
    • Table Saw for ripping boards to width and angle cuts.
    • Circular Saw (with a guide) or Jigsaw (for curves/cutouts if no table saw).
  • Drilling & Fastening:
    • Drill/Driver with bits for pilot holes, countersinking, and screwdriver bits.
    • Pocket Hole Jig (e.g., Kreg Jig) if using pocket hole joinery.
    • Brad Nailer (optional).
  • Clamping: Wood clamps (F-clamps, bar clamps).
  • Sanding: Orbital sander (or sanding block).
  • Router (Optional): For decorative edge profiling or smooth cutouts.

Step 3: Cut the Wood Pieces

  1. Safety First: Always wear safety glasses and hearing protection when operating power tools.
  2. Cut Top and Base Panels: Cut these to your exact length and width. Ensure cuts are perfectly square.
  3. Cut Side Supports:
    • Cut two rectangular pieces first, to the maximum height and depth of your stand.
    • Mark the angle for your top surface. For example, if your stand is 8" deep and you want a 20-degree angle, measure how much the back needs to be taller than the front (8" * tan(20°) ≈ 2.9").
    • Use a table saw or circular saw with a guide to cut the top edge of each side support at the desired angle.
    • Alternatively, if your design uses a simple trapezoid for the sides, cut the top and bottom angles accordingly.
  4. Cut Front Lip: Cut this piece to the exact length of your top panel and your desired height.
  5. Cut Cable/Airflow Holes (Optional): If you plan for these, use a jigsaw or router to cut them into the top panel or base panel now.
  6. Initial Sanding: Lightly sand all cut edges and faces (120-grit) to remove any splinters or rough spots. It's easier to sand individual pieces now.

Step 4: Assemble the Side Supports to the Base

This forms the foundational structure.

  1. Mark Positions: On the top surface of the base panel, mark the exact locations where the side supports will attach. They should be set back from the front edge of the base, and typically inset slightly from the sides for a cleaner look.
  2. Choose Joinery:
    • Pocket Holes (Recommended): Drill pocket holes along the bottom edge of each side support.
    • Screws & Glue: Apply glue and drive screws up from the bottom of the base into the side supports (pre-drill pilot holes and countersink).
  3. Apply Glue & Fasten:
    • Apply a thin, even bead of wood glue to the mating edges of the side supports and the base.
    • Position the side supports according to your marks.
    • Secure with screws (pocket hole screws or regular wood screws from underneath).
  4. Check for Squareness: Use a combination square to ensure the side supports are perpendicular to the base.
  5. Wipe Excess Glue: Immediately wipe away any glue squeeze-out with a damp cloth.
  6. Allow to Dry: Let the glue dry completely (per manufacturer's instructions).

Step 5: Attach the Top Panel

  1. Apply Glue: Apply a thin, even bead of wood glue along the top angled edges of the side supports.
  2. Position Top Panel: Carefully place the top panel onto the glued edges, ensuring it is flush with the side supports and the front/back edges.
  3. Choose Joinery:
    • Pocket Holes (Recommended): Drill pocket holes on the underside of the side supports (where they meet the top) before attaching, or if you prefer, through the top into the side supports (and fill later).
    • Screws & Glue: Drive screws down from the top panel into the side supports (pre-drill pilot holes and countersink). If you plan to paint or use wood filler, this is acceptable.
    • Brad Nails & Glue: Can be used for a quick assembly, but screws provide more strength.
  4. Secure: Drive screws or nails to fasten the top panel.
  5. Wipe Excess Glue: Clean up any glue squeeze-out.
  6. Allow to Dry: Let the glue dry completely.

Step 6: Attach the Front Lip

  1. Position Lip: Apply a thin bead of wood glue along the front edge of the top panel where the lip will attach.
  2. Align & Fasten:
    • Position the front lip. It should be flush with the top panel's front edge.
    • Use small brad nails or screws driven from the top of the top panel down into the lip (pre-drill/countersink).
  3. Check Alignment: Ensure the lip is straight and evenly attached.
  4. Wipe Excess Glue: Clean up any glue squeeze-out.
  5. Allow to Dry: Let the glue dry.

Step 7: Final Sanding

Thorough sanding is essential for a smooth, professional finish.

  1. Inspect: Carefully inspect the entire laptop stand for any glue squeeze-out, rough spots, or pencil marks.
  2. Fill Holes (Optional): If you've countersunk screws or used nails, fill the holes with wood filler. Let dry, then sand flush.
  3. Initial Sanding: Start with 120-grit sandpaper (if needed) to address any remaining imperfections.
  4. Progressive Sanding: Gradually move to finer grits (180, 220) until the entire stand is smooth to the touch. Pay attention to all edges and corners.
  5. Clean: Thoroughly clean the entire stand of all sanding dust. Use a vacuum cleaner, then wipe down with a tack cloth or a slightly damp cloth followed by a dry one. Ensure no dust remains before applying finish.

Step 8: Apply Finish (Optional)

A finish protects the wood and enhances its appearance.

  1. Choose Your Finish:
    • Stain: To change the color of the wood. Apply evenly, wipe off excess.
    • Paint: Apply primer (if needed), then two thin coats of paint.
    • Clear Coat: Polyurethane, lacquer, or wax to protect the wood while maintaining its natural look.
  2. Apply According to Manufacturer Instructions: Each finish has specific application, drying, and curing instructions. Follow them diligently.
  3. Dry: Allow the finish to dry and cure completely before placing your laptop on it.

Step 9: Final Touches & Enjoy!

  1. Attach Rubber Feet (Optional): Apply small adhesive rubber feet to the underside of the base. This prevents the stand from sliding and protects your desk surface.
  2. Test Fit: Place your laptop on the stand to ensure it's stable and comfortable.
  3. Organize Cables: Utilize any cable cut-outs you made.

Your custom-built laptop stand is now ready to improve your workspace!

Building a desk organizer is a highly practical and customizable woodworking project, perfect for tidying up your workspace. This guide will outline the steps for a modular desk organizer with various compartments, allowing you to tailor it to your specific needs.

Step 1: Planning and Design

Detailed planning ensures your organizer is functional and fits your desk setup.

  1. Assess Your Needs: What do you need to organize? (Pens, pencils, paper clips, sticky notes, phone, small notebooks, USB drives, etc.)
  2. Determine Dimensions:
    • Footprint (Width x Depth): Measure the space available on your desk. Common sizes might range from 8x12 inches to 12x18 inches.
    • Height: How tall should the back be? Tall enough to hold pens/pencils (e.g., 4-6 inches).
  3. Compartment Layout:
    • Sketch it Out: Draw a top-down view of your desired layout.
    • Vertical Slots: For letters, small notebooks.
    • Square/Rectangular Bins: For pens, clips, sticky notes.
    • Drilled Holes: For individual pens/pencils (similar to a pen stand).
    • Phone Stand: A sloped slot or dedicated section.
    • Small Drawer: For hidden storage.
  4. Create a Cut List: Based on your design, list every piece of wood with its exact dimensions.
    • Base Panel (1): Width x Depth
    • Back Panel (1): Width x Height
    • Side Panels (2): Depth x Height (or slightly less height if back panel overlaps side panels)
    • Internal Dividers (as many as needed): Various sizes depending on compartment layout.
    • Optional: Drawer components, phone stand pieces, small front lip.
  5. Choose Wood:
    • Pine/Poplar: Affordable, easy to work with, good for painting or staining.
    • Hardwood (Maple, Cherry, Walnut): Durable, beautiful grain, great for a natural or stained finish.
    • Plywood (Baltic Birch, Cabinet Grade): Stable, less prone to warping, good for all components.
    • Thickness: 1/4" to 1/2" thick wood is often sufficient for desk organizers; 3/4" for a more substantial build.

Step 2: Gather Materials & Tools

Materials:

  • Wood Stock: As per your cut list (e.g., 1/2" thick pine boards or plywood).
  • Wood Glue: High-quality wood glue (e.g., Titebond II or III).
  • Fasteners (Optional, for reinforcement):
    • Small Brad Nails: (e.g., 1" or 1.25") if using a nail gun.
    • Small Wood Screws: (e.g., 3/4" or 1") for critical joints (pre-drill and countersink).
  • Sandpaper: Various grits (80, 120, 180, 220).
  • Wood Filler (Optional): To fill small gaps, screw/nail holes.
  • Finish (Optional): Stain, paint, clear coat (polyurethane, lacquer, wax).
  • Small Hardware (Optional): Drawer pull, rubber feet.

Tools:

  • Safety Gear: Safety glasses, dust mask.
  • Measuring & Marking: Tape measure, pencil, straightedge, combination square.
  • Cutting Tools:
    • Miter Saw (Chop Saw) for accurate crosscuts.
    • Table Saw (Recommended for ripping boards and precise cuts).
    • Circular Saw (with a guide) or Jigsaw (if no table saw).
  • Drilling & Fastening:
    • Drill/Driver with bits for pilot holes, countersinking, and screwdriver bits.
    • Brad Nailer (optional).
    • Drill Press (Recommended for pen holes): With Forstner bit (if drilling individual pen holes).
  • Clamping: Wood clamps (F-clamps, spring clamps).
  • Sanding: Orbital sander or sanding block.

Step 3: Cut the Wood Pieces

  1. Safety First: Always wear safety glasses and hearing protection.
  2. Cut All Panels: Using your miter saw, table saw, or circular saw, cut the base, back, side, and all internal divider pieces to their exact dimensions from your cut list. Ensure all cuts are perfectly square.
    • Tip: For internal dividers, make sure their height matches the side/back panels or is slightly lower if they sit inside a frame.
  3. Cut Angled Pieces (if applicable): If your design includes an angled phone stand slot or sloped dividers, cut these angles now.
  4. Drill Pen Holes (if applicable): If creating a dedicated section for pens with drilled holes:
    • Mark the center points for each hole on the top surface of the designated piece of wood.
    • Using a drill press (highly recommended for straight holes) and a Forstner bit (sized slightly larger than your pen), drill the holes to your desired depth.
  5. Initial Sanding: Lightly sand all cut edges and faces (120-grit) to remove any splinters or rough spots. It's easier to sand individual pieces now than after assembly.

Step 4: Assemble the Main Carcass

This forms the basic outer box of your organizer.

  1. Attach Side Panels to Base:
    • Apply a thin, even bead of wood glue along the bottom edges of the two side panels.
    • Position them on the base panel, flush with the side edges and the back edge.
    • Secure with small brad nails or screws driven up from the bottom of the base panel into the side panels (pre-drill pilot holes and countersink if using screws).
  2. Attach Back Panel:
    • Apply wood glue along the back edge of the base panel and the back edges of the side panels.
    • Position the back panel, ensuring it's flush with the sides and top edges.
    • Secure with brad nails or screws (pre-drill/countersink) into the base and sides.
  3. Check for Squareness: Use a combination square to ensure all corners are 90 degrees. Make adjustments before the glue fully sets.
  4. Wipe Excess Glue: Immediately wipe away any glue squeeze-out with a damp cloth.
  5. Allow to Dry: Let the assembled carcass dry completely (per glue manufacturer's instructions).

Step 5: Install Internal Dividers and Optional Features

  1. Position Dividers: Mark the locations for each internal divider on the base panel and (if they extend that high) on the back panel.
  2. Attach Dividers:
    • Apply wood glue to the bottom and back edges of each divider.
    • Carefully position each divider according to your marks.
    • Secure with small brad nails or screws driven from the bottom of the base panel into the divider, and/or from the back panel into the divider.
    • If dividers meet side panels, secure them there too.
  3. Add Optional Features (e.g., Phone Stand Slot, Small Tray/Drawer):
    • Phone Stand: If using a pre-cut angled piece, glue and nail/screw it into position.
    • Small Tray/Drawer: Build a simple box for the tray/drawer. For a drawer, you'll need to cut a front panel, side panels, and a bottom. Assemble these and then build a simple runner system if desired, or simply let it slide in and out of its compartment.

Step 6: Final Sanding

Thorough sanding is essential for a smooth, pleasant feel.

  1. Inspect: Carefully inspect the entire desk organizer for any glue squeeze-out, rough spots, or pencil marks.
  2. Fill Holes (Optional): If you've countersunk screws or used nails, fill the holes with wood filler. Let dry, then sand flush.
  3. Initial Sanding: Start with 120-grit sandpaper (if needed) to address any remaining imperfections.
  4. Progressive Sanding: Gradually move to finer grits (180, 220) until the entire organizer is smooth to the touch. Pay attention to all edges and inside the compartments.
  5. Clean: Thoroughly clean the entire organizer of all sanding dust. Use a vacuum cleaner, then wipe down with a tack cloth or a slightly damp cloth followed by a dry one. Ensure no dust remains before applying finish.

Step 7: Apply Finish (Optional)

A finish protects the wood, makes it easier to clean, and enhances its appearance.

  1. Choose Your Finish:
    • Stain: To change the color of the wood. Apply evenly, wipe off excess.
    • Paint: Apply primer (if needed), then two thin coats of paint.
    • Clear Coat: Polyurethane, lacquer, or wax to protect the wood while maintaining its natural look.
  2. Apply According to Manufacturer Instructions: Each finish has specific application, drying, and curing instructions. Follow them diligently.
  3. Dry: Allow the finish to dry and cure completely before placing items inside.

Step 8: Final Touches & Organize!

  1. Add Small Hardware (Optional): Attach any drawer pulls or rubber feet to the bottom of the organizer to prevent sliding and protect your desk.
  2. Fill it Up: Place your pens, pencils, sticky notes, and other desk items into their new, custom-built home. Enjoy your organized workspace!
1

Building a desk organizer is a highly practical and customizable woodworking project, perfect for tidying up your workspace. This guide will outline the steps for a modular desk organizer with various compartments, allowing you to tailor it to your specific needs.

---

### **Step 1: Planning and Design**

Detailed planning ensures your organizer is functional and fits your desk setup.

1.  **Assess Your Needs:** What do you need to organize? (Pens, pencils, paper clips, sticky notes, phone, small notebooks, USB drives, etc.)
2.  **Determine Dimensions:**
    *   **Footprint (Width x Depth):** Measure the space available on your desk. Common sizes might range from 8x12 inches to 12x18 inches.
    *   **Height:** How tall should the back be? Tall enough to hold pens/pencils (e.g., 4-6 inches).
3.  **Compartment Layout:**
    *   **Sketch it Out:** Draw a top-down view of your desired layout.
    *   **Vertical Slots:** For letters, small notebooks.
    *   **Square/Rectangular Bins:** For pens, clips, sticky notes.
    *   **Drilled Holes:** For individual pens/pencils (similar to a pen stand).
    *   **Phone Stand:** A sloped slot or dedicated section.
    *   **Small Drawer:** For hidden storage.
4.  **Create a Cut List:** Based on your design, list every piece of wood with its exact dimensions.
    *   **Base Panel (1):** Width x Depth
    *   **Back Panel (1):** Width x Height
    *   **Side Panels (2):** Depth x Height (or slightly less height if back panel overlaps side panels)
    *   **Internal Dividers (as many as needed):** Various sizes depending on compartment layout.
    *   **Optional:** Drawer components, phone stand pieces, small front lip.
5.  **Choose Wood:**
    *   **Pine/Poplar:** Affordable, easy to work with, good for painting or staining.
    *   **Hardwood (Maple, Cherry, Walnut):** Durable, beautiful grain, great for a natural or stained finish.
    *   **Plywood (Baltic Birch, Cabinet Grade):** Stable, less prone to warping, good for all components.
    *   *Thickness:* 1/4" to 1/2" thick wood is often sufficient for desk organizers; 3/4" for a more substantial build.

---

### **Step 2: Gather Materials & Tools**

**Materials:**

*   **Wood Stock:** As per your cut list (e.g., 1/2" thick pine boards or plywood).
*   **Wood Glue:** High-quality wood glue (e.g., Titebond II or III).
*   **Fasteners (Optional, for reinforcement):**
    *   Small Brad Nails: (e.g., 1" or 1.25") if using a nail gun.
    *   Small Wood Screws: (e.g., 3/4" or 1") for critical joints (pre-drill and countersink).
*   **Sandpaper:** Various grits (80, 120, 180, 220).
*   **Wood Filler (Optional):** To fill small gaps, screw/nail holes.
*   **Finish (Optional):** Stain, paint, clear coat (polyurethane, lacquer, wax).
*   **Small Hardware (Optional):** Drawer pull, rubber feet.

**Tools:**

*   **Safety Gear:** Safety glasses, dust mask.
*   **Measuring & Marking:** Tape measure, pencil, straightedge, combination square.
*   **Cutting Tools:**
    *   Miter Saw (Chop Saw) for accurate crosscuts.
    *   Table Saw (Recommended for ripping boards and precise cuts).
    *   Circular Saw (with a guide) or Jigsaw (if no table saw).
*   **Drilling & Fastening:**
    *   Drill/Driver with bits for pilot holes, countersinking, and screwdriver bits.
    *   Brad Nailer (optional).
    *   **Drill Press (Recommended for pen holes):** With Forstner bit (if drilling individual pen holes).
*   **Clamping:** Wood clamps (F-clamps, spring clamps).
*   **Sanding:** Orbital sander or sanding block.

---

### **Step 3: Cut the Wood Pieces**

1.  **Safety First:** Always wear safety glasses and hearing protection.
2.  **Cut All Panels:** Using your miter saw, table saw, or circular saw, cut the base, back, side, and all internal divider pieces to their exact dimensions from your cut list. Ensure all cuts are perfectly square.
    *   *Tip: For internal dividers, make sure their height matches the side/back panels or is slightly lower if they sit inside a frame.*
3.  **Cut Angled Pieces (if applicable):** If your design includes an angled phone stand slot or sloped dividers, cut these angles now.
4.  **Drill Pen Holes (if applicable):** If creating a dedicated section for pens with drilled holes:
    *   Mark the center points for each hole on the top surface of the designated piece of wood.
    *   Using a drill press (highly recommended for straight holes) and a Forstner bit (sized slightly larger than your pen), drill the holes to your desired depth.
5.  **Initial Sanding:** Lightly sand all cut edges and faces (120-grit) to remove any splinters or rough spots. It's easier to sand individual pieces now than after assembly.

---

### **Step 4: Assemble the Main Carcass**

This forms the basic outer box of your organizer.

1.  **Attach Side Panels to Base:**
    *   Apply a thin, even bead of wood glue along the bottom edges of the two side panels.
    *   Position them on the base panel, flush with the side edges and the back edge.
    *   Secure with small brad nails or screws driven up from the bottom of the base panel into the side panels (pre-drill pilot holes and countersink if using screws).
2.  **Attach Back Panel:**
    *   Apply wood glue along the back edge of the base panel and the back edges of the side panels.
    *   Position the back panel, ensuring it's flush with the sides and top edges.
    *   Secure with brad nails or screws (pre-drill/countersink) into the base and sides.
3.  **Check for Squareness:** Use a combination square to ensure all corners are 90 degrees. Make adjustments before the glue fully sets.
4.  **Wipe Excess Glue:** Immediately wipe away any glue squeeze-out with a damp cloth.
5.  **Allow to Dry:** Let the assembled carcass dry completely (per glue manufacturer's instructions).

---

### **Step 5: Install Internal Dividers and Optional Features**

1.  **Position Dividers:** Mark the locations for each internal divider on the base panel and (if they extend that high) on the back panel.
2.  **Attach Dividers:**
    *   Apply wood glue to the bottom and back edges of each divider.
    *   Carefully position each divider according to your marks.
    *   Secure with small brad nails or screws driven from the bottom of the base panel into the divider, and/or from the back panel into the divider.
    *   *If dividers meet side panels, secure them there too.*
3.  **Add Optional Features (e.g., Phone Stand Slot, Small Tray/Drawer):**
    *   **Phone Stand:** If using a pre-cut angled piece, glue and nail/screw it into position.
    *   **Small Tray/Drawer:** Build a simple box for the tray/drawer. For a drawer, you'll need to cut a front panel, side panels, and a bottom. Assemble these and then build a simple runner system if desired, or simply let it slide in and out of its compartment.

---

### **Step 6: Final Sanding**

Thorough sanding is essential for a smooth, pleasant feel.

1.  **Inspect:** Carefully inspect the entire desk organizer for any glue squeeze-out, rough spots, or pencil marks.
2.  **Fill Holes (Optional):** If you've countersunk screws or used nails, fill the holes with wood filler. Let dry, then sand flush.
3.  **Initial Sanding:** Start with 120-grit sandpaper (if needed) to address any remaining imperfections.
4.  **Progressive Sanding:** Gradually move to finer grits (180, 220) until the entire organizer is smooth to the touch. Pay attention to all edges and inside the compartments.
5.  **Clean:** Thoroughly clean the entire organizer of all sanding dust. Use a vacuum cleaner, then wipe down with a tack cloth or a slightly damp cloth followed by a dry one. Ensure *no* dust remains before applying finish.

---

### **Step 7: Apply Finish (Optional)**

A finish protects the wood, makes it easier to clean, and enhances its appearance.

1.  **Choose Your Finish:**
    *   **Stain:** To change the color of the wood. Apply evenly, wipe off excess.
    *   **Paint:** Apply primer (if needed), then two thin coats of paint.
    *   **Clear Coat:** Polyurethane, lacquer, or wax to protect the wood while maintaining its natural look.
2.  **Apply According to Manufacturer Instructions:** Each finish has specific application, drying, and curing instructions. Follow them diligently.
3.  **Dry:** Allow the finish to dry and cure completely before placing items inside.

---

### **Step 8: Final Touches & Organize!**

1.  **Add Small Hardware (Optional):** Attach any drawer pulls or rubber feet to the bottom of the organizer to prevent sliding and protect your desk.
2.  **Fill it Up:** Place your pens, pencils, sticky notes, and other desk items into their new, custom-built home. Enjoy your organized workspace!
